  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_35e258ef-3f86-493c-afda-2f6d037c0499'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10053966167165692607">An entrancing, otherworldly collection of short stories from one of Europe's most accomplished 20th century writers, new to Penguin Modern Classics<br/>A counter-prophet attempts the impossible to prove his power; a girl sees the hideous fate of her sisters and father in a mirror bought from a gypsy; the death of a prostitute causes an unanticipated uprising; and the lives of e</span>
  <span id="freeText10053966167165692607" style="display:none">An entrancing, otherworldly collection of short stories from one of Europe's most accomplished 20th century writers, new to Penguin Modern Classics<br />A counter-prophet attempts the impossible to prove his power; a girl sees the hideous fate of her sisters and father in a mirror bought from a gypsy; the death of a prostitute causes an unanticipated uprising; and the lives of every ordinary person since 1789 are recreated in the almighty Encyclopedia of the Dead. These stories about love and death, truth and lies, myth and reality range across many epochs and settings. Brilliantly combining fact and fiction, epic and miniature, horror and comedy, this was Danilo Kiš final work, published in Serbo-Croatian in 1983.<br /><br />Kiš is one of the great European writers of the post-war period - Guardian<br />Compulsively readable - Daily Telegraph <br /><br />Fantasy chases reality and reality chases fantasy. Pirandello and Borges are not far away. But these names are intended as approximate references. Kiš is a new, original writer - Times Literary Supplement <br /><br />Intense and exotic, his mysteries hint at unspeakable secrets that remain forever beyond the story-teller's grasp - Boyd Tonkin<br /><br />Danilo Kiš was born in the then Kingdom of Yugoslavia in 1935. After an unsettled childhood during the Second World War, in which several of his family members were killed, Kiš studied literature at the University of Belgrade where he lived for most of his adult life. He wrote novels, short stories and poetry and went on to receive the prestigious NIN Award for his novel Pešcanik. He died in Paris in 1989.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10053966167165692607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2328692327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2328692327" />
    <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea"><img alt="Amalia Gkavea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559158764p2/56789747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2328692327?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalia Gkavea" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea">Amalia Gkavea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/34518368-the-encyclopedia-of-the-dead">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=serbia">serbia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=balkan-literature">balkan-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2328692327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3751099507037324185"><i>
  <b>‘’When a lie repeats for a long time, people begin to believe.’’</b>
</i><br/><br/>This collection is an ode to the values of Literature. An opportunity to contemplate, to feel, to reflect. A beautiful, haunting symphony composed of religion, philosophy, folklore, living History. A work that places the human being, naked and feeble as we are, at the heart of every story. Our fears, our fickleness, our virtues and vices, our ability to create only to destroy. Our desire to rebuild and then tear down everything </span>
  <span id="freeText3751099507037324185" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>‘’When a lie repeats for a long time, people begin to believe.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />This collection is an ode to the values of Literature. An opportunity to contemplate, to feel, to reflect. A beautiful, haunting symphony composed of religion, philosophy, folklore, living History. A work that places the human being, naked and feeble as we are, at the heart of every story. Our fears, our fickleness, our virtues and vices, our ability to create only to destroy. Our desire to rebuild and then tear down everything and everyone. Our search for knowledge and love to an unknown end...These are some of the most beautiful, poignant stories you’ll ever read….<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’I offer you knowledge and the desert.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>Simon Magus:</b>
</i> A story set after the Resurrection of Christ. Simon is a man who doesn’t believe, fed up by the large number of disciples, irritated by the new belief. He wants to prove that there isn’t only one truth in the world but a multitude of personal ‘’truths’’. He encounters Peter and accepts a challenge. Well, he should have known better...Gnosticism is a major influence in this poignant tale whose conclusion is open to many interpretations.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’It was a beautiful gray November evening with streetlamps flickering in the mist.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>Last Respects:</b>
</i> The death of a prostitute provides the ground for an act of rebellion as people whom society has pushed to the margins strip the graves off their flowers to cover the last residence of poor Mariette. Set in Hamburg during the early 20th century, full of images of a cruel and yet strangely romantic era…<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’In that distant year of 1929, one approached Belgrade via the Sava Bridge, probably with the same joy of arrival as one feels today.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>The Encyclopedia of the Dead: </b>
</i> A woman, who has recently lost her father, travels to Stockholm. In the Royal Library, she discovers the volumes of an encyclopedia whose entries are dedicated to every ordinary human being. As she starts reading about her father, we follow her thoughts and perhaps begin to contemplate on the significance of all of us. Most of us will remain unknown after our death. No entry in any encyclopedia will be dedicated to us. And yet, aren’t ordinary people the ones who lead History to her eternal circle? What would the ones in power do if it weren’t for their simple, common followers? What if we come to think of the story as a parable for a troubled past, a fragile present and an uncertain future? <br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’Was that, too, a dream?’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>The Legend of the Sleepers:</b>
</i> This story is based on the legend of the 7 Sleepers, the Roman noblemen who converted to Christianity while in the court of Decius, an emperor of pagan beliefs.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’This story does not begin abruptly, in medias res, but gradually, as when night falls in the woods.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>The Mirror of the Unknown:</b>
</i> For me, this is the jewel of the collection. The youngest girl of a Jewish family in Hungary during the 19th century witnesses a tragic moment of the future through a looking glass bought from a gypsy. A heartbreaking story loaded with political, historical and social connotations.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’If I understood correctly, there is nothing left.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>The Story of the Master and the Disciple:</b>
</i> A tale set in Prague with reference to Kabbalah and Mysticism and with the ingratitude of a mediocre disciple towards his gifted master.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’At that moment, as if by command, the drums began to beat, as sinister and monotonous as rain.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>Pro Patria Mori:</b>
</i> Most of us are taught that we are obliged to die to defend our country. To what end? In our story, a young delusional aristocrat awaits his execution. His mother’s sole purpose is to save appearances. A tragic fable on the futility of politics, ambitions and the eternal shedding of innocent blood for some ‘’higher’’ purpose that constantly changes…<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’We hear terrified cries for help, then a few isolated shots finishing off one of the children. And then the heavy silence of the Siberian night.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>The Book of Kings and Fools:</b>
</i> A harrowing story of religion, revolution, propaganda. Of atrocities, despair and the never ending idiocy of humans to believe everything they’re told to, to live according to their prejudice…<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’But I do not matter, I do not matter in the least.’’</b>
</i><br /><i>
  <b>Red Stamps with Lenin’s Head:</b>
</i> An unnamed woman claims to be the lover of a famous deceased poet and condemns all critics of his work through a letter to his biographer.<br /><br />A collection about deception, misconception, futility, violence and the darkness we sadistically created in ourselves and in the lives of those we love. A writer that was taken too soon….<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’History is written by the victors. Traditions are woven by the people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />...and in these four sentences lies the whole essence of the blood-crazed world we’ve created through centuries….<br /><br />My reviews can also be found on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://theopinionatedreaderblog.wordpress.com">https://theopinionatedreaderblog.word...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3751099507037324185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3529877466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3529877466" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3529877466?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=fables">fables</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=serbian-authors">serbian-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3529877466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2996648634181838427">A collection of nine short stories, fables, and almost-essays by a Serbian writer. In an unusual Postscript (in my Penguin Books edition), the author gives us a detailed description of how each story or essay and came about and what it meant. <br/><br/>The title story, “The Encyclopedia of the Dead,” is the best-known work in the book. In a Borges-like fantasy a young woman leans about her father from a one-volume encyclopedia that somehow contains all the details of the lives of common people. Well-known</span>
  <span id="freeText2996648634181838427" style="display:none">A collection of nine short stories, fables, and almost-essays by a Serbian writer. In an unusual Postscript (in my Penguin Books edition), the author gives us a detailed description of how each story or essay and came about and what it meant. <br><br>The title story, “The Encyclopedia of the Dead,” is the best-known work in the book. In a Borges-like fantasy a young woman leans about her father from a one-volume encyclopedia that somehow contains all the details of the lives of common people. Well-known persons who have biographies are excluded. The point is “…redressing human injustices and granting all God’s creatures an equal place in eternity.” The volume gives intimate details of each person’s entire life in a way that becomes almost cinema-like as it is read. She learns the secret of her father’s late-in-life acquisition of drawing skill and how it coincided with his death from cancer. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1599141457i/30056323._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The story “Simon Magus” is set in Samara 15 years after Christ was crucified. Simon performs magic tricks to draw crowds in villages and competes with apostles Perter and John for the attention of the crowds. He argues with Peter about why he is promoting such a nasty God – just look around you. God violates all his own commandments such as Thou Shall Not Kill: “Killing is his vocation.” <br><br>In The “Mirror of the Unknown” a young girl’s father and two sisters are traveling by coach. She sees in advance their murder by highway bandits in her mirror bought from a “Gypsy.” <br><br>The story “The Book of Kings and Fools” is an extensive imaginative essay, partly based in fact, about how the notorious anti-Semitic book “The Protocols of the Elders of Zion” came about and how much damage it caused through the ages. And it continues to cause damage: the author adds a personal note that the book was “…directly responsible for a hunting rifle’s being fired at the windows of our house.” [He had Jewish ancestry from his father.] <br><br>“To Die for One’s Country is Glorious” tells the story of an imprisoned main awaiting execution for his part in a political uprising. “History is written by the victors. Legends are woven by the people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain.” <br><br>In “Red Stamps with Lenin’s Picture” the author has some fun with the literary establishment. An elderly woman attends a lecture about a famous poet given by a man who wrote a biography of the poet. The speaker expounds about the tragedy of a collection of the poet’s lost letters and he goes on about interpretations of his poems by a famous literary critic. Afterwards, the elderly lady, who went unnoticed in the audience, writes to the biographer telling him how she was the poet’s life-long mistress and that SHE burned the letters. She points out the foolishness of all the interpretations of his poems that she knows were odes to HER. <br><br>Good stories and good writing. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1599141457i/30056324.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Kis (1935-1989) was of multiple backgrounds. Ethnically, he was a Hungarian-Montenegrin-Serb and Jewish but was raised as an Orthodox Christian. He wrote in Serbo-Croatian, the official language of the former Yugoslavia. The authors primarily influencing his work were Borges, Nabokov and Bruno Schultz. (Coincidentally the last GR review I posted was of my first Bruno Schultz work: The Street of Crocodiles.) <br><br>Photo of a library in Lisbon by Ivo Rainha, Upslash, posted on discoverwalks.com<br>A stamp from Montenegro honoring the author, from Wikipedia <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="2996648634181838427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2606570236" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2606570236" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2606570236?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23385697-glenn-russell?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2606570236" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11743950880563671423"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1543667554i/26673764._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Internationally acclaimed Serbian author Danilo Kiš and his family lived through some of the harshest and nightmarish years of the twentieth century. His best-known books are the novel <i>Hourglass</i> and two collections of short stories: <i>A Tomb for Boris Davidovich</i> and the collection under review. All nine tales in <i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> are stunning, highly literary and deeply moving. But rather than offer observations of a general nature, I'll focus on the title piece since my heart softened </span>
  <span id="freeText11743950880563671423" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1543667554i/26673764._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Internationally acclaimed Serbian author Danilo Kiš and his family lived through some of the harshest and nightmarish years of the twentieth century. His best-known books are the novel <i>Hourglass</i> and two collections of short stories: <i>A Tomb for Boris Davidovich</i> and the collection under review. All nine tales in <i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> are stunning, highly literary and deeply moving. But rather than offer observations of a general nature, I'll focus on the title piece since my heart softened and became progressively more tender with each sentence - nay, I'll go further: in all the many works of fiction I've read over the years, I've not encountered one more heartfelt. <br><br><i>THE ENCYCLOPEDIA OF THE DEAD</i> <br>During his visit to Sweden at the invitation of the Institute for Theater Research, one evening following a performance of August Strindberg's <i>Ghost Sonata</i>, the narrator's host escorts him to the Royal Library. It's nearly midnight and the building is closed but no matter - his host, Kristina Johansson, flashes a special pass to the disgruntled guard at the door permitting him entry. At this point Mrs. Johanssen bids her guest goodnight, leaving him to conduct his visitation solo. <br><br>What follows leads a reader to suspect this twenty-five page tale of Danilo Kiš might be more akin to Borges dreamtiger than Balzac realism since the library strikes the narrator as a dungeon, the guard a Cerberus and each of the rooms houses books arranged alphabetically - after visits to the A, B, C and D rooms, following a premonition, the narrator breaks into a run, wherein we read: "Agitated and out of breath, I arrived at the letter M and <i>with a perfectly clear goal in mind</i> opened one of the books. I had realized - perhaps I had read about it somewhere - that this was the celebrated <i>Encyclopedia of the Dead</i>." <br><br>The first thing he sees is the text's one and only illustration, exactly the same one he keeps on his desk, a photograph of his father taken in 1936 immediately following his discharge from military service with his father's name and the years 1910-1979 in parenthesis underneath. And to think his father died just weeks prior to his Sweden trip. One big reason he took this visit in the first place was to escape his grief, a futile gesture, he admits, since travel doesn't help - we bear our grief within ourselves.<br><br>I wish I could mail a copy of this book to everyone reading my review so the story could be read in its entirety. Since this is not possible, I'll shift to commenting on particular passages as a way of spotlighting the actual words of Danilo Kiš:<br><br>"When I saw that I might go on reading until dawn and be left without any concrete trace of what I had read for either me or my mother, I decided to copy out several of the most important passages and make a kind of summary of my father's life." ---------- Isn't that what we all do with the loved ones in our life that have passed on? We assemble our memories into a tapestry of scenes, some vivid, others that fade around the edges, some other that are difficult to bring into focus and remain blurs, creating our own mini version of the <i>Encyclopedia</i>. <br><br>"What makes the <i>Encyclopedia</i> unique (apart from its being the only existing copy) is the way it depicts human relationships, encounters, landscapes - the multitude of details that make up a human life." ---------- This is precisely why biographies are critically important and are frequently bestsellers. Herodotus writes his highly readable, entertaining and insightful <i>Histories</i>, however with Plutarch and Suetonius there is a giant step forward in appreciating the details of a single human life. And again beginning with Boswell's <i>Life of Samuel Johnson</i> such appreciation is brought more in accord with modern sensibilities. <br><br>"The only condition - something I grasped at once, for inclusion in <i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> is that no one whose name is recorded here may appear in any other encyclopedia." ----------- Ah, Danilo Kiš recognizes those people living ordinary lives, so called, have their own dramas worthy of being recorded and appreciated, even honored and revered. <br><br>"The sea he glimpsed, for the first time, at twenty-five, from the slopes of the Velebit on April 28, 1935, would reside within him - a revelation, a dream sustained for some forty years with undiminished intensity, a secret, a vision never put into words." ---------- The <i>Encyclopedia</i> explicates not only the facts of his father's life, but equally or even more importantly, his fathers dreams and visions. In this way, the <i>Encyclopedia</i> expands out to the vistas of imaginative literature, most appropriate since Danilo himself is a spinner of language chimeras we term fiction. <br><br>"By the same token, and in keeping with the logic of their program (that there is nothing insignificant in a human life, no hierarchy of events), they entered all our childhood illnesses - mumps, tonsillitis, whooping cough, rashes - as well as a bout of lice and my father's lung trouble." ---------- <i>The Encyclopedia</i> does not overlook or thoughtlessly cast aside those all-consuming bouts of pain and illness. Thus, Danilo's <i>Encyclopedia</i> even outpaces a conventional biography. <br><br>"For <i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i>, history is the sum of human destinies, the totality of ephemeral happenings. That is why it records every action, every thought, every creative breath, every spot height in the survey, every shovelful of mud, every motion that cleared a brick from the ruins." ---------- That's why I have never been attracted to history textbooks - too general. What textbook on twentieth century history would begin to document the daily suffering of my own father who grew up in an orphanage, hit the Normandy beach the first day of D-Day and toiled as a shift worker at a chemical factory for thirty years? <br><br>"He would curse God, heaven, earth, the Russians, the Americans, the Germans, the government, and all those responsible for granting him such a miserable pension after he had slaved a lifetime, but most of all he cursed television, which, insolent to the point of insult, filled the void of his evenings by bringing into the house the grand illusion of life." ---------- Quite the telling observation by a sensitive literary artist - as torturous, humiliating and demanding as his father's life was over the years, the biggest insult dad must deal with is that cultural cesspool, the ever-present television with its unending stream of drivel. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1543672618i/26674137._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>“Thanks to suffering and madness, I have had a finer, richer life than any of you, and I wish to go to my death with dignity, as befits the great moment after which all dignity and majesty cease. Let my body be my ark and my death a long floating on the waves of eternity." - Danilo Kiš<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1529102247i/25794209.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Danilo Kiš, 1935-1989</span>
  <a data-text-id="11743950880563671423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2074475941" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2074475941" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2074475941?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2074475941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3888023798519265071">There is truth and there is faith and they don’t mix – like oil and water…<br/><blockquote>When a lie is repeated long enough, people start believing it. Because people need faith.</blockquote><br/><i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> is an obvious attempt to follow in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges' title='Jorge Luis Borges' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jorge Luis Borges</a>’ footsteps, and although the stories are good and mostly inventive they lie on the other plane.<br/><i>Simon Magus</i> is a gnostic tale with an apparent sympathy for the advocates of Gnosticism. <i>Last Respects</i> is a flowery fable of a cocotte’s interment. <i>The Encyclop</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3888023798519265071" style="display:none">There is truth and there is faith and they don’t mix – like oil and water…<br /><blockquote>When a lie is repeated long enough, people start believing it. Because people need faith.</blockquote><br /><i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> is an obvious attempt to follow in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Jorge Luis Borges</a>’ footsteps, and although the stories are good and mostly inventive they lie on the other plane.<br /><i>Simon Magus</i> is a gnostic tale with an apparent sympathy for the advocates of Gnosticism. <i>Last Respects</i> is a flowery fable of a cocotte’s interment. <i>The Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> is an ironic variation on the library of Babel. <i>The Legend of the Sleepers</i> is a dreamlike parable of dream, life and death… <br /><blockquote>Who can draw a sharp line between sleep and death?<br />Who, O Lord, can draw a sharp dividing line between present, past, and future?<br />Who, O Lord, can separate the joy of love from the sadness of memory?</blockquote><br /><i>Red Stamps with Lenin’s Head</i>, which <b>Danilo Kiš</b> called a pure fiction, I enjoyed most, it is a sarcastic story erasing the borderline between reality and myth.<br />All the stories are united with the subject of death.<br />Death is a final metaphysical act of life…</span>
  <a data-text-id="3888023798519265071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141396989" href="/book/show/24874294-the-encyclopedia-of-the-dead">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3250759-paul-fulcher?shelf=2019">2019</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2808805116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4607652085348089081"><i>History is written by the victors. Legends are woven by the people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain.</i><br/><br/>A vitally important book of short stories that deserves to be ranked alongside Borges finest, translated by Michael Henry Heim, and with an illuminating postscript by the author Danilo Kiš himself.<br/><br/>This review from Nick Lezard (Guardian - bring him back!) sums it up well:<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/jun/02/encyclopedia-of-the-dead-danilo-kis-review-fitting-tribute'>https://www.theguardian.com/books/201...</a><br/><br/>and Wikipedia (sorry!) has a good overview of the stories<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Encyclopedia_of_the_Dead'>https://en.wikipedia.or</a></span>
  <span id="freeText4607652085348089081" style="display:none"><i>History is written by the victors. Legends are woven by the people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain.</i><br /><br />A vitally important book of short stories that deserves to be ranked alongside Borges finest, translated by Michael Henry Heim, and with an illuminating postscript by the author Danilo Kiš himself.<br /><br />This review from Nick Lezard (Guardian - bring him back!) sums it up well:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/jun/02/encyclopedia-of-the-dead-danilo-kis-review-fitting-tribute">https://www.theguardian.com/books/201...</a><br /><br />and Wikipedia (sorry!) has a good overview of the stories<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Encyclopedia_of_the_Dead">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Enc...</a><br /><br />The title story is a masterpiece but my personal favourites were Simon Magus and the shortest of them all, Pro Patria Mori, which, featuring in Javier Cercas's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44666922.Lord_of_All_the_Dead" title="Lord of All the Dead by Javier Cercas" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of All the Dead</a> initially drew me to this collection.<br /></span>

<div id="review_32156456" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32156456" />
    <a title="Jennifer (JC-S)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/283014-jennifer-jc-s"><img alt="Jennifer (JC-S)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528085840p2/283014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32156456?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer (JC-S)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer (JC-S)" href="/user/show/283014-jennifer-jc-s">Jennifer (JC-S)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780571148202" href="/book/show/3789381-encyclopaedia-of-the-dead">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32156456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9639163043257101250"><br/><br/>I’ve not previously read Mr Kis’s work and I was not sure what to expect. I read this collection in translation (by Michael Henry Heim). This was the first book I could obtain, and I was totally swept up in the beauty of the prose from beginning to end. This collection of nine stories touches on a number of facets of life: relationships, encounters and experiences. Each is unique. Each illustrates a different aspect of existence, including questioning the notion of divine order.<br/><br/>‘Everything a liv</span>
  <span id="freeText9639163043257101250" style="display:none"><br /><br />I’ve not previously read Mr Kis’s work and I was not sure what to expect. I read this collection in translation (by Michael Henry Heim). This was the first book I could obtain, and I was totally swept up in the beauty of the prose from beginning to end. This collection of nine stories touches on a number of facets of life: relationships, encounters and experiences. Each is unique. Each illustrates a different aspect of existence, including questioning the notion of divine order.<br /><br />‘Everything a living man can know of death.’<br /><br />Because of these differences, I suspect that each story could be my favourite on a different day or read. Each provides food for thought and the language is exquisite. On this read, I particularly enjoyed ‘Simon Magus’ and his questioning of divine order, ‘To Die for One’s Country Is Glorious’ describing the final hours of Esterhazy, and the reading journey of the bereaved daughter in the title story. <br /><br />In fewer than 200 pages, Mr Kis has managed to evoke a set of experiences and reactions that linger on in the mind. Where does life end, and death begin? Are the boundaries mutable or immutable? We will each have (or form) our own private views on this question. For myself, I am delighted to have read this book and will be looking to read more of Mr Kis in translation.<br /><br />‘History is written by victors. Legends are woven by people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain.’<br /><br />And now, I need to read more.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9639163043257101250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_445940236" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/445940236" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445940236?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445940236" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7849473924297968451">November 1, 2012 All Saints' Day. Death looks very much like the ending of a book. It is inevitable, inescapable, final, often unpredictable yet necessary and common to all. Each human life that ends is like a book that has been read, and was loved, and is kept in at least one other person's memory. For a book, its author or its first reader; for a person, his/her mother or someone who had loved him/her most.<br/><br/>All Saints' Day is a celebration and commemoration of sequels, or the possibility thereo</span>
  <span id="freeText7849473924297968451" style="display:none">November 1, 2012 All Saints' Day. Death looks very much like the ending of a book. It is inevitable, inescapable, final, often unpredictable yet necessary and common to all. Each human life that ends is like a book that has been read, and was loved, and is kept in at least one other person's memory. For a book, its author or its first reader; for a person, his/her mother or someone who had loved him/her most.<br /><br />All Saints' Day is a celebration and commemoration of sequels, or the possibility thereof, based on the conviction or hope that a life story does not end after its last page. Stories are immortal. Books end and people, die but their stories go on. Heroes and martyrs were less potent when they were alive than after they had died. In life, they were just of ordinary flesh and blood; in death, they become legends which become stronger as the passing time makes them more dead. <br /><br />Death is, in fact, itself a story which can be told in many ways. Danilo Kis did it here, in this collection of nine short death-themed stories. This triumph of the unkillable over the one who kills inspired me to create my own mini-tale: Death--the arrogant champion--was so angry at Danilo Kis for making It like just a mere prop for stories that when Kis was about to be awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature he died. What a pain in the ass! It is unlikely that Death reads, or listens to, stories. Out of disdain, perhaps, to the one It cannot conquer. Or maybe It is just a moron completely devoid of logic, morals, compassion or practicability. It blindly strikes anywhere, anytime. Thus sometimes it's horrific, dramatic, serene, brutal, senseless, sudden, silent, screaming, timely, heroic, holy, historic and even sometimes unknown. Spiteful, too, for there can be no other explanation why Kis--so deserving of the Nobel--was not given a bit more life to be justly honored. <br /><br />Ironically, this collection is practically a paean to Death. My favorite is the one entitled "To Die for One's Country is Glorious", having just read the biography of Dietrich Bonhoeffer (his biographer briefly wonders if Bonhoeffer, on the eve of his execution, was able to sleep) and having Jose Rizal as my country's national hero (before his brief walk towards the place of his execution by firing squad a Spanish doctor examined his his pulse and was amazed that it was normal). Here, we have a condemned man, a young count named Esterhazy (I'm sure he was one of the illustrious forebears Peter Esterhazy mentioned in his Celestial Harmonies, a 1001 book).On his last night, just before his execution, he couldn't sleep but pretends to sleep. In the morning, he steels himself and grits his teeth to show control and outward calmness. He makes sure his hands do not tremble and reins in "the cowardly behavior of his intestines and solar plexus." He wants to show a brave front because he belongs to a family with a glorious name and with a long line of heroes before him.<br /><br />A few days before, his mother paid him a last visit. Secretly, she told him that she will beg the Emperor for a last-minute pardon. She will be there at his scheduled hanging. If she's wearing black, that means she had failed; but if she's in white, that would mean he will be saved at the last minute.<br /><br />As Esterhazy marches along the boulevard with his guards, a hate-filled crowd jeers him and threw a couple of stones at him. Crushed, he was forced into the posture of the defeated. Seeing that his courage seems to be leaving him, the crowd cheers. But then, in a balcony, he sees his mother wearing a family heirloom, an all-white dress worn as a wedding dress of one of their ancestors in an Imperial wedding. Immediately, almost insolently, he straightens up and shows the crowd how brave an Esterhazy he is in the face of a certain death. Up to the last minute, as the hangman finally removes the stool from under his feet, he was still expecting a more dramatic reprieve (which never came). Danilo Kis summed everything up as follows:<br /><br /><br />"There are two possible conclusions. Either the young aristocrat died a brave and noble death, fully conscious of the certainty thereof, his head held high, or the whole thing was merely a clever bit of playacting directed by a proud mother. The first, heroic, version was upheld and promulgated--orally, and then in writing, in their chronicles--by the sans-culottes and Jacobins; the second, according to which the young man hoped to the very end for some magical sleight of hand, was recorded by the official historians of the powerful Habsburg dynasty to prevent the birth of a legend. History is written by the victors. Legends are woven by the people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain."<br /><br /><br />Only death is certain. Bravery and heroism can be doubted. But not Death.<br /><br />"Simon Magus," like Peter of the New Testament, was a preacher and the setting of the story bearing his name is seventeen years after Jesus Christ's death and resurrection. He performs his own miracles. But he preaches the exact opposite of Peter's gospel: that God is a tyrant and the author of all the world's ills. In a debate with Peter--again with two versions--it is once more demonstrated that even in matters of faith nothing is written in stone. Only Death is certain.<br /><br />A beloved whore dies of pneumonia in "Last Respects." During her burial, in a cemetery for the poor, the reader feels the valiant effort of her Ukrainian sailor-lover to give her a final glowing tribute losing out to the inexorable annihilation that Death brings.<br /><br />In "The Legend of the Sleepers" three Christian martyrs lay inside a dark cave: Dionysius, his friend Malchus and John with his dog Qitmir. After several centuries Dionysius awakens. The past, present and future assault his senses and all he could do is to ask repeatedly: are these just dreams? Towards the end of the story one sees Death triumphant, Dionysius still in the darkness of the cave--<br /><br /><br />"vainly straining his eyes, vainly calling to his friend Malchus, vainly calling to John, the saintly shepherd, vainly calling to the green-eyed dog Qitmir, vainly calling to the Lord his God: the darkness was as thick as tar, the silence--the silence of the tomb of eternity."<br /><br /><br />No person dies in "The Story of the Master and the Disciple" but one sees here the teachings and reputation of a master slain by his own disciple whom he had unwittingly misled by his own philosophies. Satire at its best, and the funniest of all the pieces here.<br /><br /><br /> "The Book of Kings and Fools" would get one into thinking how so-called ancient books, with uncertain origins (most likely mundane), are given supernatural auras and become catalysts for wars, revolutions and similar self-righteous false crusades which feed Death with a rich harvest of souls, mostly innocent ones.<br /><br /><br />"Red Stamps with Lenin's Pictures" is a parody of literary critics and scholars especially those who like to pick on works left by dead writers. Death seems to be an impregnable wall here and anyone who attempts to pass through it suffers ridicule.<br /><br /><br />In "The Mirror of the Unknown" a young girl watches her father and two sisters murdered in the woods as it happens through a mirror bought from a Gypsy, another aspect of the mystery of Death, as it is roughly based on a true story like the other stories in this compilation(so the postscript says).<br /><br /><br />Lastly, the title story, "The Encyclopedia of the Dead." The massive Encyclopedia here is a record of everything about each person who had ever lived: "every action, every thought, every creative breath, every spot height in the survey, every shovelful of mud, every motion that cleared a brick from the ruins." But what for? Its mysterious compiler seems to have a central message: that each individual is unique and sacred, and that we are all novel, unrepeatable masterpieces of creation.<br /><br />Yet Death will claim us all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7849473924297968451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42326109?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15368616832128174257">If for whatever reason you haven't read anything by Danilo Kis yet, I'm gonna go ahead and say "Do so as soon as possible." Jewish guy from what was Yugoslavia at the time, wrote in Serbo-Croatian, and as good as anybody you'd care to name. Really just top shelf. You can start anywhere because all the books are good. This is stories, loosely linked by the theme of death. Kis's three big topics are death, childhood, and the Holocaust, and yeahyeah, heavy stuff, and generally pretty depressing but</span>
  <span id="freeText15368616832128174257" style="display:none">If for whatever reason you haven't read anything by Danilo Kis yet, I'm gonna go ahead and say "Do so as soon as possible." Jewish guy from what was Yugoslavia at the time, wrote in Serbo-Croatian, and as good as anybody you'd care to name. Really just top shelf. You can start anywhere because all the books are good. This is stories, loosely linked by the theme of death. Kis's three big topics are death, childhood, and the Holocaust, and yeahyeah, heavy stuff, and generally pretty depressing but he is simply not going to make it easier on anyone by resorting to cliches and the man can just flat out write so well that he could get you with an essay about donuts if he wanted to. It isn't pretty but it sure is worth it.</span>

<div id="review_450526966" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/450526966" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/450526966?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140132663" href="/book/show/1488369.The_Encyclopedia_of_the_Dead">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=philosophical">philosophical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=macabre">macabre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=collection">collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=serbian">serbian</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer450526966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5641729058572088064">"Only death is certain" seems to be the dominant message of the nine short stories in this collection by Yugoslov writer, Danilo Kis. My first time to read a work by him and I am now craving for more.<br/><br/>Although the stories are about death and dying, the book is neither terrifying nor nightmarish. So, if you are looking to be scared like when you read a Stephen King or a Clive Barker, this book is not for you. This book borders on being philosophical about highly inventive scenes that only gifted a</span>
  <span id="freeText5641729058572088064" style="display:none">"Only death is certain" seems to be the dominant message of the nine short stories in this collection by Yugoslov writer, Danilo Kis. My first time to read a work by him and I am now craving for more.<br /><br />Although the stories are about death and dying, the book is neither terrifying nor nightmarish. So, if you are looking to be scared like when you read a Stephen King or a Clive Barker, this book is not for you. This book borders on being philosophical about highly inventive scenes that only gifted authors could put up as milieus in short stories. Take for example my favorite story in the book, "To Die for One's Country is Glorious," I think that Kis was able to give a good tip on how to die: playacting. A young Esterhazy (that seems like a surname of a royal family in Hungary) is about to die by hanging and he is so composed because he is expecting that there is something that will save him because his beautiful mom is wearing a heirloom gown that she donned during a family wedding.<br /><br />There were other stories that made this book unputdownable for me. The opening one, entitled "Simon Magus" about Jesus disciples, was a stunner and I went right into the next one somehow expecting that they would be great too. I was not really disappointed but I got bored at some point until the story "The Encyclopedia of the Dead" came and whoa, I said to myself, this is the storyline that I have not read anywhere despite me having read so many fiction books. It is a good way to tell a biography and having that twist in the end is brilliant. <br /><br />Three fantastic stories out of 9. But that's my personal taste. I am sure that you would love the others too. Bottom line, I am just happy to have finished a book by a Yugoslav writers. Thank God for the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die. This book seems to be a good way to prepare oneself for dying and it is not even inspirational (imagine one of the Jesus disciples praying for his detractor to fall from the sky) but rather realist and I would say, self help (playacting is a good way to go).</span>
  <a data-text-id="5641729058572088064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review450526966">

<span id="freeTextContainer13067637507454846904">An intriguing short collection of stories and fables. This was a book I picked up just because of the title, though the Penguin Modern Classics series generally guarantees quality. The title story is reminiscent of and probably influenced by Borges, though the dream in question, of a sect that creates an encyclopedia documenting the lives of ordinary people in minute detail, was based on a real dream related by Kis's wife.   <span id="freeText13067637507454846904" style="display:none">An intriguing short collection of stories and fables. This was a book I picked up just because of the title, though the Penguin Modern Classics series generally guarantees quality. The title story is reminiscent of and probably influenced by Borges, though the dream in question, of a sect that creates an encyclopedia documenting the lives of ordinary people in minute detail, was based on a real dream related by Kis's wife. Some of the other stories are re-tellings and fictionalisations of historical events and legends - once again these evoke Borges and his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17717.Labyrinths_Selected_Stories_and_Other_Writings" title="Labyrinths Selected Stories and Other Writings by Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Labyrinths: Selected Stories and Other Writings</a>. Finally the author has added his own fascinating postscript on the ideas and sources behind each of his stories.</span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8305009204299939174" style="display:none">"My rage to love overlooks death like a window on the courtyard"<br />The edition I have been reading contains 9 short stories by Danilo Kis and a wonderful Postscript that I encourage anyone who has this edition to read.<br />Kis has a tremendous gift and every time I read one of his works I feel transported and completely immersed in the story. 'The Encyclopaedia of the Dead' is a dream and one that I wish I could also have. Kis is describing any reader's ideal experience. 'The Encyclopaedia of the Dead' was his last work of fiction giving it a valedictory, almost testamentary feeling. <br />All the other 8 stories are well worth reading but if I had to choose one would be 'The Book of Kings and Fools' an assay/romance of the infamous 'Protocol of the Elders of Zion'.<br />Probably the best quote is to be found in 'Simon Magus':"Where everyone lies, no one lies; where everything is lies, nothing is a lie.<br />⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️ HIGHLY RECOMMENDED⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_81452264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81452264" />
    <a title="Jake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1821338-jake"><img alt="Jake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229910175p2/1821338.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81452264?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jake" href="/user/show/1821338-jake">Jake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1821338-jake?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81452264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4880190415439010954">I love Borges and after I exhausted his books, I went looking for other authors who were influenced by him. Danilo Kis came up near the top of the list, so I picked up "Encyclopedia of The Dead", his book of short stories. And indeed, Kis' subjects are Borgesian: gnostic heretics, infinite encyclopedias, men condemned by dictators, Koranic legends, reviews of imaginary books. In many ways, Kis is a better writer than Borges— the stories have the mournful lyricism of Milan Kundera at his best. Bu</span>
  <span id="freeText4880190415439010954" style="display:none">I love Borges and after I exhausted his books, I went looking for other authors who were influenced by him. Danilo Kis came up near the top of the list, so I picked up "Encyclopedia of The Dead", his book of short stories. And indeed, Kis' subjects are Borgesian: gnostic heretics, infinite encyclopedias, men condemned by dictators, Koranic legends, reviews of imaginary books. In many ways, Kis is a better writer than Borges— the stories have the mournful lyricism of Milan Kundera at his best. But this book is no match for "Ficciones" or "Labyrinths" or any of Borges' collections. I think that's because what really animates Borges' stories are the ideas— the dizzying, utterly unique ideas that you can't find in the work of any other author. Kis just doesn't have those— and without them, all that's left are beautiful (but not mindbending) stories about love, loss, and death. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4880190415439010954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11744202962894462577">(4.5 stars.) My third stop on the Danilo Kiš train, after the masterpiece (<i>Garden, Ashes</i>) and the semi-autobiographical first novel (<i>The Attic</i>) . . . <i>Encyclopedia of the Dead</i> is very good, almost in five-star territory through the first few stories but then the influence of Borges became perhaps too obvious. The prose is really something else, though; uniquely, densely poetic while also ineffably airy/playful, a tossed-off first draft that succeeds every time.</span>

<div id="review_1508056797" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1508056797" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15416206-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595873317p2/15416206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1508056797?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/15416206-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141396989" href="/book/show/24874294-the-encyclopedia-of-the-dead">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15416206-paul?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15416206-paul?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1508056797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10848142098194045036">I am a fan of Borges, and much of my MA dissertation focused on the disintegration of the Yugoslav state in the midst of conflicting nationalist rhetoric; imagine my delight when I discovered Kiš.<br/><br/>Sadly, great expectations are usually dashed, and I've just put this volume down feeling a little cold. It's hit and miss - my favourite stories were Simon Magus, the Encyclopedia of the Dead and the Mirror of the Unknown. At best though, these are sub-Borges, mostly lacking Jorge Luis' wit and charm th</span>
  <span id="freeText10848142098194045036" style="display:none">I am a fan of Borges, and much of my MA dissertation focused on the disintegration of the Yugoslav state in the midst of conflicting nationalist rhetoric; imagine my delight when I discovered Kiš.<br /><br />Sadly, great expectations are usually dashed, and I've just put this volume down feeling a little cold. It's hit and miss - my favourite stories were Simon Magus, the Encyclopedia of the Dead and the Mirror of the Unknown. At best though, these are sub-Borges, mostly lacking Jorge Luis' wit and charm that balanced out the high flying intellectualism. Kiš also seems to be uncomfortable with the out-and-out fantastical: for example, after setting up the wonderfully bibliographic hypothesis that forms the premise of the titular story, he can't help but reveal it all to be a dream at the end. A disappointing cliché.<br /><br />As for the rest of the stories: forgettable, boring. Entries like the Legend of the Sleepers and the Book of Kings and Fools are bogged down by the stylistic constraints Kiš sets himself - biblical, journalistic, distracting him from the dullness of the overall execution. Other stories seem to reflect thinly veiled personal vendettas, such as Red Stamps with Lenin's Head and the Story of the Master and Disciple. I read that Kiš was in a bitter place during the writing of this collection, recovering from accusations of plagiarism in his previous novel and loving estranged from his wife. Some authors may have used this to artistic advantage, but in Kiš' case it just leaves a sour note.<br /><br />The collection is not without merit. I enjoyed the recurring themes of death and mythology, and what seems to be Kiš' take on semiotics (stories within stories, unreliable narration, etc.). It has its own important place in the study of nationalisms and their mythologies - for example, Pro Patria Mori is a pithy fable that ruminates on the formation and role of political myth and folk tradition as opposed to 'official' documentation.<br /><br />As a whole, the stories are interesting on an intellectual level only. The glimpses into fantastical worlds are too reluctant and half-formed to merit favourable comparison with Borges. I may delve more into Kiš, but for now I'll more likely look into other Yugoslav authors of the era. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10848142098194045036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_257437210" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/257437210" />
    <a title="Begemoth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4921294-begemoth"><img alt="Begemoth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311807376p2/4921294.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/257437210?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Begemoth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Begemoth" href="/user/show/4921294-begemoth">Begemoth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4921294-begemoth?shelf=read-two-times">read-two-times</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer257437210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer433975886838019659">This book, like Mahler's Symphony no. 9, has a central theme of the dead. And, also like Mahler's Symphony, raises many questions, but leave to the reader(listener) to find the answers for himself, since there is no unique and unified approach on solving the problem of death and its overcoming. In this precious book we see nine ways of trying to overcome the death, which are told through nine stories. They raise many unpleasant questions, and the basic one is: Can a human being achieve the immor</span>
  <span id="freeText433975886838019659" style="display:none">This book, like Mahler's Symphony no. 9, has a central theme of the dead. And, also like Mahler's Symphony, raises many questions, but leave to the reader(listener) to find the answers for himself, since there is no unique and unified approach on solving the problem of death and its overcoming. In this precious book we see nine ways of trying to overcome the death, which are told through nine stories. They raise many unpleasant questions, and the basic one is: Can a human being achieve the immortality? Sometimes, it seems that neither working of miracles, encyclopediasm, literature itself, defamation, nor plagiarism can help in achieving immortality. And the one approach that seems to be the most powerful, again like in Mahler's Symphony no. 9, is achieving the immortality through the power of love. But again, there is no general conclusion. The reader is leaved to think of it, maybe forever, armed with his knowledge and education. This book is for sure one of the diamonds of the Serbian literature, and the book that is read at least once in life.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3588755520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3588755520" />
    <a title="Andy " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11331153-andy"><img alt="Andy " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455636729p2/11331153.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3588755520?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy " href="/user/show/11331153-andy">Andy </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3588755520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5018647007658123863">You can see the influence of Borges (with a bit of Bruno Schulz) most prominently in the style of these stories. The best of these are open-ended and thought-provoking, others have political overtones without being didactic. Kis writes with a sort of resigned pessimism, evoking moments of awe and wonder but ultimately ending with what feels like world-weariness.<br/><br/>My favorite story was "The Book of Kings and Fools," an alternate history of "The Protocols of the Elders of Zion." The parallels with t</span>
  <span id="freeText5018647007658123863" style="display:none">You can see the influence of Borges (with a bit of Bruno Schulz) most prominently in the style of these stories. The best of these are open-ended and thought-provoking, others have political overtones without being didactic. Kis writes with a sort of resigned pessimism, evoking moments of awe and wonder but ultimately ending with what feels like world-weariness.<br /><br />My favorite story was "The Book of Kings and Fools," an alternate history of "The Protocols of the Elders of Zion." The parallels with the conspiratorial thinking we see spreading like wildfire today is genuinely frightening. When I closed the book on that tale I was in a bit of a daze. The other standouts are the ghostly "The Mirror of the Unknown" and the heavily 'Borgesian' "The Encyclopedia of the Dead." <br /><br />I enjoyed most of these, but only a couple of them were especially memorable and I would hesitate to easily recommend this. Also, don't read the introduction first, it spoils the stories.<br /><br /><br />Simon Magus - This seems to be an allegory for the failure of defiance in the face of overwhelming power, crowd hysteria and delusion. Some years after the death of Christ a man travels around Palestine preaching about the horrors of the world, and tyranny of an evil God. <br /><br />Last Respects - Wow, this is an excellent story, it's quite short and rather uneventful, but the description used and moods evoked are quite good. It is by turns tragic and hilarious. A revolutionary gives a eulogy at the grave of a prostitute. <br /><br />The Encyclopedia of the Dead - This is cited as the best story in the book, I liked a couple others more, but it's certainly one of the most memorable. This one evokes a lot of thoughts and emotions about all the small, seemingly trivial things that make up a life, and also the act of reading itself. A woman discovers a book in which every aspect of a person's life, regardless how minor are recorded in detail. <br /><br />The Legend of the Sleepers - This is a slower, denser story than most here, and although some parts are quite plodding I enjoyed the very dream-like atmosphere. I can see the Schulz influence, parts of this reminded me of that nebulous fiber-thin moment when you drift and dip in and out between consciousness and dream. Three Christians entombed by the pagan emperor lie in sleep for centuries and are one day resurrected. <br /><br />The Mirror of the Unknown - Walter de la Mare, not Borges, not Schulz, but de la Mare is the author that came to mind while reading this excellent tale. Perhaps it's just coincidence, in any case I thought this was one of the very best stories in the book. This is cited in the introduction as "Kis’s only venture into the supernatural" which is a real shame! It is a tale of presaged tragedy, also told in an effectively vague style. <br /><br />The Story of the Master and the Disciple - I think any reading of this would have to admit it's a dense and uneventful story, not entirely without interest but seemingly purposefully baffling. I actually re-read this one and other than finding parts of it more humorous, I can't say much for it. An author who attempts to reconcile art and morality takes on a disciple who rebels against him. <br /><br />Pro Patria Mori - This is the shortest story, about an execution with a thought-provoking conclusion. Once again we have a political theme of the individual crushed in the face of a larger power, in this case even their memory after death. <br /><br />The Book of Kings and Fools - Damn! Reading parts of this was downright eerie considering all the conspiratorial thinking circulating today. This is a sort of alternate history of "The Protocols of the Elders of Zion," and far too much of what is written here feels prescient. This is a leisurely-paced, perhaps a bit over-long or rambling story (it is the longest story), but it was one of my favorites.<br /><br />Red Stamps with Lenin’s Head - This seems to be a reflection on the futility of overly academic interpretation, but is also related to a real event in the life of Kis himself. A longtime lover of a famous poet offers a new interpretation of everything he has written.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5018647007658123863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
