    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_356535578" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356535578" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/660474-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203721329p2/660474.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/356535578?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/660474-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140132663" href="/book/show/1488369.The_Encyclopedia_of_the_Dead">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/660474-james?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/660474-james?shelf=magical-realism-serbia">magical-realism-serbia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/660474-james?shelf=fiction-serbia">fiction-serbia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer356535578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4236095749906758776">I had high hopes for this collection of stories I've been holding onto for a few years now. Truthfully, I made it through three-quarters of the collection and found myself left with a sense of unemotional, flavorless philosophizing. That's not to say that philosophical writing doesn't have it's place, but I read for emotional resonance, so unless the intellectual lifting is pretty damn impressive, I'm probably going to get bored, which I did. The title story and "The Mirror of the Unknown" were </span>
  <span id="freeText4236095749906758776" style="display:none">I had high hopes for this collection of stories I've been holding onto for a few years now. Truthfully, I made it through three-quarters of the collection and found myself left with a sense of unemotional, flavorless philosophizing. That's not to say that philosophical writing doesn't have it's place, but I read for emotional resonance, so unless the intellectual lifting is pretty damn impressive, I'm probably going to get bored, which I did. The title story and "The Mirror of the Unknown" were a lot closer to the kind of story I'm into, but I'm sorry to say that Mr. Kis did not win me over.<br /><br />Note: This is also a work in translation, so it's possible that reading it in the L1 would have a significantly different impact. Grain of salt and all that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4236095749906758776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1732191065" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1732191065" />
    <a title="Mila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55523193-mila"><img alt="Mila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584186285p2/55523193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1732191065?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mila" href="/user/show/55523193-mila">Mila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788496834590" href="/book/show/6507041-enciclopedia-de-los-muertos">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55523193-mila?shelf=stories">stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55523193-mila?shelf=balkans">balkans</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1732191065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2051438992507001892">Kiš follows a line of culturally cosmopolitan authors, epitomised in the twentieth century by Borges - or later Yourcenar or Bolaño - and going back to Goethe whose West-eastern Divan he planned to quote ironically in this Encyclopedia. <br/>The frames for his stories are borrowed from fictional as well as historical sources - the Bible, the Quran, some minor news items or the greatest tragedies in European history. Events take place from the early years after Christ’s death to the post-war twentieth</span>
  <span id="freeText2051438992507001892" style="display:none">Kiš follows a line of culturally cosmopolitan authors, epitomised in the twentieth century by Borges - or later Yourcenar or Bolaño - and going back to Goethe whose West-eastern Divan he planned to quote ironically in this Encyclopedia. <br />The frames for his stories are borrowed from fictional as well as historical sources - the Bible, the Quran, some minor news items or the greatest tragedies in European history. Events take place from the early years after Christ’s death to the post-war twentieth century, jumping back and forth in time. Characters travel from Ukraine to France, from Belgrade to Sweden, from Prague to Istanbul, Central Europe being the pivot between the western and the eastern cultural spheres. The fate of Central European Jews throughout twentieth century is somehow ever underlying, which also supports the pervasiveness of the theme of death.<br /><br />Kiš makes the claims that every human life is irreducibly unique, constituted by an infinity of details that link a being to his landscape. Discourse is only an attempt to capture this uniqueness, as well as to pay tribute to the dead, in the antique tradition of the poetic tomb. The many flowers between the pages are also a sign of this intention. But texts are unreliable- when not dangerous- and there lies Kiš’s formidable pessimism.<br />“History is written by the victors. Legends are woven by the people. Writers fantasize. Only death is certain.”<br />Nothing transcends death. Death is the only universal parameter and every drop of life is always already infused with death-obsession. Even love and especially love is inextricably linked to it.<br /><br />“Simon Magus” gives two versions of the death of the protagonist without judging the truth of each. Precisely, Simon is a character who rebels and preaches against the vanity of religious dogma, which is helpless against evil, hatred and death. However, his own compassion for those who are suffering allows him to levitate only a brief moment, not enough not to crash to the ground and meet his own end. <br />In the “Enciclopedia of the dead”, the narrator finds the tale of her father's life in a magical book that contains the unique story of every single life after 1789. For the reader, there is however a double filter blocking the access to this life: the Enciclopedia’s article and the narrator’s synthesis of this article. <br />“The Story of the Master and the Disciple” ponders the ancient question of wether ethics and aesthetics are compatible. The disciple tries to seize the absurdity of life in a text whose vanity he would rather better conceal than get rid of. As the Master is not able to point out the difference between a real moral scope and the sheer appearance of one, he also gets annulled as an authority, like Simon.<br />“The Book of King and Fools” recounts the trajectory of the most infamous fake book in history, “the Protocols of the Elders of Zion”. This is one of the most powerful stories I ever read about Holocaust. Its power lies in the contrast between the neutral, even detached investigation about the book and the silent violence of the inserted witnessed scenes of pogroms. One on the Russian side, one of the German side and, in the middle, as a pivot, a gun shot on the narrator’s house, stated as an immediate repercussion of the killer-book’s diffusion. All the all-too-signifying names are held at a distance. “The Protocols” become “The Conspiracy” and the two most famous protagonists of twentieth century European history become “the amateur painter” and “the anonymous Georgian seminarist”. Everything in the story is true, warns the narrator, except the snow, as it is the evocative literary element that cannot be missing in a Russian story. There are actually much more literary echoes: Nilus gets seized by the beard like the old captain in “the Brothers Karamazov”. Rachkovsky seems right out of “the Demons”. The Nabokovian melancholy of Russian emigrants carrying their books, or dwelling in hotels abroad is superbly pictured. This is a good illustration of how Kiš blends historical sources with literary imagination. He is overall excellent at evocative imagery and his summoning of the deserts, rocks and fierce sunlight of the Middle-East is equally gripping.<br /><br />The mirror is another important device in the stories. Unlike the Borgesian mirrors which reproduce the living in a horrifying way, Kiš’s mirrors reflect what is hidden, especially violent death, like in “Mirror of the Unknown” where the young daughter of a Jewish merchant foresees the brutal murder of her father and sisters. Likewise, the corpses of other murdered Jews, glimpsed in a mirror in Odessa, “like quotes from a book”. <br />This reasserts an old equivalence between book and mirror. The book tends to be a faithful reflexion of the specificity of each life, but it is also a tribute, a tomb-shaped mirror. It reflects unknown dead’s lives, and thereby is also a mirror for the reader to contemplate his own mortal condition.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2051438992507001892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1371461903" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1371461903" />
    <a title="Stephen Curran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11677504-stephen-curran"><img alt="Stephen Curran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574349718p2/11677504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1371461903?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Curran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Curran" href="/user/show/11677504-stephen-curran">Stephen Curran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141396989" href="/book/show/24874294-the-encyclopedia-of-the-dead">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11677504-stephen-curran?shelf=read-from-penguin-mod-classics-list">read-from-penguin-mod-classics-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1371461903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13079550121255298447">Some 'pocket-sized novels' on the theme of death.<br/><br/>1. Simon Magus. <br/><br/>Based on an gnostic tale from The Acts of Peter. A prophet eruditely denounces the Christian God and is confronted by Peter and the apostles. His attempts at performing miracles (levitation and being buried alive: the story is told twice) both end in a gruesome death, which his companion takes as proof that the prophet was right: God is a tyrant. It's a story that manages to be funny yet deadly serious; allegorical yet ambiguous.<br/><br/>2.</span>
  <span id="freeText13079550121255298447" style="display:none">Some 'pocket-sized novels' on the theme of death.<br /><br />1. Simon Magus. <br /><br />Based on an gnostic tale from The Acts of Peter. A prophet eruditely denounces the Christian God and is confronted by Peter and the apostles. His attempts at performing miracles (levitation and being buried alive: the story is told twice) both end in a gruesome death, which his companion takes as proof that the prophet was right: God is a tyrant. It's a story that manages to be funny yet deadly serious; allegorical yet ambiguous.<br /><br />2. Last Respects.<br /><br />A giddy, drunken account of the raucous funeral of a Ukrainian prostitute. Flowers abound.<br /><br />3. The Encyclopaedia of the Dead (A Whole Life)<br /><br />A vast library contains all the facts pertaining to the lives of everyone whose names do not appear in any other encyclopaedia. Thanks to this profoundly egalitarian endeavour, a grieving daughter reads the full account of the life of her recently deceased father, and learns of the forces of history which inevitably shaped it.<br /><br />4. The Legend of the Sleepers<br /><br />A vivid retelling of a resurrection story from the Qur'an. Repetitious and poetic, it reads almost like an incantation. "And he felt the joy of his body after the dank, slimy, viscid shell of darkness has fallen away, the childlike joy of the flesh, of entrails and bones, a joy of bone marrow and brain marrow, a bestial amphibian joy, a reptilian joy..."<br /><br />5. The Mirror of the Unknown<br /><br />A child witnesses the violent death of her family in an apparently magical hand-mirror.<br /><br />6. The Story of the Master and the Disciple<br /><br />A writer mentors, and is then betrayed by, a student.<br /><br />7. Pro Patria Mori<br /><br />(The title means 'to die for one's country', or so Google tells me). A man goes to the gallows in a display of what is either aristocratic bravery, or unwarranted hopefulness. It ends with: "Only death is certain."<br /><br />8. The Book of Kings and Fools<br /><br />A history of The Conspiracy, a book with more than a passing resemblance to the real-life anti-Semitic hoax The Protocols of the Elders of Zion. A fundamentally evil work, "one of the best textbooks ever composed for modern dictators or anybody aspiring to be one", it corrupts and inspires murder wherever it is found.<br /><br />9. Red Stamps with Lenin's Head<br /><br />Writing to a lecturer about the fate of a famous poet's letters, a young woman corrects the theories of academic critics and claims that his poems are, in truth, about the 'long, passionate, terrible romance' she shared with him. A moving, clever and empathetic portrait of grief.<br /><br />... All of which might make The Encyclopedia of the Dead sound rather heavy-going and academic, when it is, in fact, touching, compassionate and occasionally funny. Any effort it might demand is thoroughly rewarded.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13079550121255298447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1374593732?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/1935343-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140132663" href="/book/show/1488369.The_Encyclopedia_of_the_Dead">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1374593732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11588579566910054084">I had high hopes for this collection of short stories. The cover blurb states that "this is one of the finest fantastic collections since Borges's 'Ficciones'". In some respects, I can see how this applies - the stories often blur fact and fiction, contain conundrums, and are full of semi-legitimate literary references which cross-reference each other within each story. However, they lack the playfulness that I find in Borges. This means - to be blunt - many of them are just boring: "The Book Of</span>
  <span id="freeText11588579566910054084" style="display:none">I had high hopes for this collection of short stories. The cover blurb states that "this is one of the finest fantastic collections since Borges's 'Ficciones'". In some respects, I can see how this applies - the stories often blur fact and fiction, contain conundrums, and are full of semi-legitimate literary references which cross-reference each other within each story. However, they lack the playfulness that I find in Borges. This means - to be blunt - many of them are just boring: "The Book Of Kings And Fools" for example is relentless in its monotony. What is particularly frustrating is that I feel the ideas are here if they had been rendered in a more interesting way. "Simon Magus" is a great opener and "Red Stamps With Lenin's Picture" is a great closure - what these have in common is interesting characterisation. Too often the other pieces fell by my personal wayside because they trod the 'literary' side of the 'fantastic' too dully, as though mothballed in a dead encyclopaedia (so to speak). Flicking through the book whilst doing this review I wonder if I've been a little harsh, but I feel that's simply because the book reads better glanced at rather than read. I suspect there's a story there, somewhere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11588579566910054084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    if (submit_form) {

      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_11553583'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11553583-prokleta-avlija?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Prokleta avlija<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivo Andrić<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.30 avg rating &mdash; 7,169 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1954\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17547000787751117789\">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)\n\nThe novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17547000787751117789\" style=\"display:none\">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)\n\nThe novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother of the Sultan Bajazet, who ruled Turkey in the fifteenth century. Ćamil, in his isolation, comes to believe that he is Džem, and that he shares his evil destiny: he is born to be a victim of the State. Because of his stories about Džem’s ambitions to overthrow his brother, Ćamil is arrested under suspicion of plotting against the Sultan. He is taken to a prison in Istanbul, where he tells his story, to Petar, a monk.\n\nOut of these exotic materials, Andrić has constructed a book of great clarity, brevity and interest. No doubt it will be read by some as a political parable about the tyranny of the State, but also as a quite simply story about ill-fortune and human misunderstanding, fear and ignorance. Džem and Ćamil are doomed – and the certainty of their persecution is sometimes relieved, sometimes intensified by the stupidity and fright of the people who cross their ill-starred lives.\n\nConstruction takes up most of the book’s space: the central story of Džem as related by Ćamil lasts only a chapter or two. For the rest of the time the reader strips layer off layer, as one narrator passes him on the next. There is an interesting passage that helps to explain this method, at the moment when Ćamil starts narrating Džem’s story in the first person. “I” is a word, we are told, which fixes the position of the speaker in such a way that the exercise of will is no longer possible, and the speaker strength is exceeded – strength, presumably, to break out of the identification that all his past actions and thoughts force upon him when he uses the word. “I” is both a confession and an imprisonment. The fact that the novel passes the reader on from one narrator to the next rather suggests that the author is taking constant evasive action, lest he betray himself or his reader into the kind of “personal confession” which seals the fate of Ćamil. What exactly this game of form flirting with meaning signifies, must be left to the individual reader.\n\nThe movement is centripetal, towards Džem’s story, and then disperses. Details within the story are made to mimic this form. Thus when Peter receives the message telling him of his impending release:\n\n“Two younger prisoners...were chasing around using him as the centerpoint of ever narrowing circles. Annoyed, he tried to break away from these exuberant youths when one of them brushed against him and he felt a folded scrap of paper thrust into his hand. The youths continued their chase but now in widening circles...”\n\nThe reader is led on just such a chase in the course of the novel. The effect of this is to make the plot seem more like a poetic image than an ordinary plot: capable, therefore, of as many meanings as are the images of an allusive poem. Yet the language is simple and direct, not at all “poetic”. The characters are remarkable alive, even in conversation. Karađoz, the governor of the goal, is a spidery authoritarian, who loves to torment the charges he loves. The prisoners “complained about the way one complains about one’s life and curses one’s destiny...it would have been hard for them to imagine life without him”.\n\n“The Devil’s Yard” is justified, as all symbolic and figurative novels must be, by the extent to which it touches the emotions. It is extremely moving. Fear, horror, despair, amusement at times – all these indicate that the threat of the meaning has been recognized.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17547000787751117789\" href=\"#\" It recounts the story of Sheikh Nuruddin, a dervish residing in an Islamic monastery in Sarajevo in the eighteenth century during the Ottoman Turk hegemony over the Balkans. When his brother is arrested, he must descend into the Kafkaesque world of the Ottoman authorities in his search to discover what happened to him. He narrates his story in the form of an elaborate suicide note, regularly misquoting the Koran. In time, he begins to question his relations with society as a whole and, eventually, his life choices in general. //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1743453'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1743453.Gospo_ica?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Gospođica<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivo Andrić<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.20 avg rating &mdash; 1,508 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1945\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8228697298076263980\">Gospođica (The Woman from Sarajevo) by Andrić was originally published in 1945. and is one of the three novels that make up the \"Bosnian Trilogy\". The other two are Bosnian Chronicle and The Bridge on the Drina.\n\nThe novel is set in the cities of Sar<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8228697298076263980\" style=\"display:none\">Gospođica (The Woman from Sarajevo) by Andrić was originally published in 1945. and is one of the three novels that make up the &quot;Bosnian Trilogy&quot;. The other two are Bosnian Chronicle and The Bridge on the Drina.\n\nThe novel is set in the cities of Sarajevo and Belgrade during the first three decades of this century. The places and time are not incidentally chosen. Ivo Andrić is from Bosnia and knows the people and their problems in this unique area. The theme and composition suggest a work of modern classicism. It is the tragic life of a woman disappointed in people and in the world she lives in. She is completely enslaved by money, in which she hopes to find security and revenge in a hateful and insecure world. At the same time she is literally and feverishly following her bankrupt father’s last plea, as she becomes not only thrifty but a real miser in a classical Gogolian style.\n\nThis novel is dominated by a single character, a spinster named Miss Raika Radaković who is dominated by a single passion, stinginess. The development of this passion is traced through the first third of this century, from the time Miss Raika’s dying father, a ruined merchant, solemnly enjoins his school-girl-daughter to guard with her life the little property he can leave her, until some thirty-five years later when she herself dies, a scrawny old woman, unloved and unlovable, but faithful to her trust. As a moneylender she parlays small insurance legacy after her father into a small fortune. But whatever her wealth at any time, she will spend none of it on herself. For this single-minded greed for money she puts everything else out of her life – love, friendship, concern for other people.\n \nRaika also speculates in currencies during the tumultuous years of the Austro-Hungarian Empire and the uncertain emergence of the new Yugoslav state. A good deal of the power of Andrić’s tale comes through the depiction of this seething historical background, in whose torrent the wretched Raika’s life is washed along like a piece of flotsam.\n\nThe novel is carefully thought out and fully imagined, with solid passages of description in the nineteenth-century manner and scrupulous attentions to the job of relating the individual life to the broader pattern of social and economical change. With brilliant economy Andrić sketches the stages through which Miss Raika hoards and increases her inheritance: Sarajevo in the era of provincial usury, the economic upheavals and disintegration of the First World War, staid Belgrade headily embarking on the jazz age when the war is over, the pinched depression years during which Miss Raika dies. The requisite drama – the testing of the ruling passion – is present in the form of a young man whose physical resemblance to a beloved uncle long dead touches Miss Raika’s heart enough to make her swerve, though only momentarily, from her objective of never parting with her money.\n\n The chronicle is Andrić’s favorite form, and this novel is no exception, a highly poetic form depicting the past and the present in a meticulous artistic and philosophical manner. Detailed historical analysis serves as a “chain” and always gives a basis for philosophical interpretation.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8228697298076263980\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/127676.Danilo_Ki_">About Danilo Kiš</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15864979327687686360">Danilo Kiš was born in Subotica, Danube Banovina, Kingdom of Yugoslavia, the son of Eduard Kiš (Kis Ede), a Hungarian Jewish railway inspector, and Milica Kiš (born Dragićević) from Cetinje, Montenegro. During the Second World War, he lost his father and several other family members, who died in various Nazi camps. His mother took him and his older sister Danica to Hungary for the duration of the </span>
  <span id="freeText15864979327687686360" style="display:none">Danilo Kiš was born in Subotica, Danube Banovina, Kingdom of Yugoslavia, the son of Eduard Kiš (Kis Ede), a Hungarian Jewish railway inspector, and Milica Kiš (born Dragićević) from Cetinje, Montenegro. During the Second World War, he lost his father and several other family members, who died in various Nazi camps. His mother took him and his older sister Danica to Hungary for the duration of the war. After the end of the war, the family moved to Cetinje, Montenegro, Yugoslavia, where Kiš graduated from high school in 1954.<br /><br />Kiš studied literature at the University of Belgrade, and graduated in 1958 as the first student to complete a course in comparative literature. He was a prominent member of the Vidici magazine, where he worked until 1960. In 1962 he published his first two novels, Mansarda and Psalam 44. Kiš received the prestigious NIN Award for his Peščanik ("Hourglass") in 1973, which he returned a few years later, due to a political dispute.<br /><br />During the following years, Kiš received a great number of national and international awards for his prose and poetry.<br /><br />He spent most of his life in Paris and working as a lecturer elsewhere in France.<br /><br />Kiš was married to Mirjana Miočinović from 1962 to 1981. After their separation, he lived with Pascale Delpech until his early death from lung cancer in Paris.<br /><br />A film based on Peščanik (Fövenyóra) directed by the Hungarian Szabolcs Tolnai is currently in post-production.<br /><br />Kiš was nominated for the Nobel Prize in Literature and was due to win it, were it not for his untimely death in 1989.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15864979327687686360" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
